Summary of Key Trends in Manufacturing AI Transformation (AX)

This analysis was conducted to identify the key trends in the recent AI Transformation (AX) of the manufacturing industry. As a result of the analysis, the most prominent trend is the spread of AI-based 'Digital Twin' technology. An approach that simulates real manufacturing processes with virtual models and augments data with generative AI to increase the accuracy of predictive maintenance and quality control has been highly evaluated. Particularly, the semiconductor industry is leading these innovations.

The second key trend is maximizing operational efficiency and productivity. AI is widely applied to specific and practical problem-solving, such as precise quality inspection based on machine vision, optimizing energy efficiency of existing (legacy) equipment, and inventory management through demand forecasting. This suggests an evolution beyond simple automation to a stage that supports data-driven optimal decision-making.

Finally, the evolution towards intelligent autonomous production systems is accelerating. Human-robot collaboration through AI collaborative robots (cobots) and the establishment of data infrastructure for autonomous production, among other things, illustrate the blueprint for future 'AI Factories'. For a successful AI transformation, securing foundational technologies such as IIoT architecture, Edge AI, and data centers is a key task, and through this, the manufacturing industry is expected to establish a more intelligent and flexible production system.

This paper presents a practical architecture that accelerates digital-physical convergence from the early stages by designing a manufacturing digital twin in parallel with generative AI (GAI) and predictive AI (PAI) modules. It recommends using GAI to synthesize rare defects and geometry variations and build a lightweight 3D testbed based on multi-view video to lower the training and validation costs and risks of PAI. In key processes like welding, combine current/voltage, thermal image, acoustic, and vision data to detect pre-defects early and provide closed-loop feedback of the results to robot/welder parameters. Ensure operational reliability through in-line NDE label matching, MES/QMS integration, edge inference, and MLOps. As an expansion roadmap, a strategy of sequentially introducing explainable/context-aware/agent-type AI, monitoring KPIs (FPY, scrap, TTD, precision, line delay), and expanding from a single-cell pilot to multi-process is effective.

This presents a practical framework that simultaneously improves quality and efficiency while reducing data scarcity and on-site risks, through an AI-digital twin design combining GAI and PAI. GAI focuses on generating synthetic defects and geometry deviations, and building a multi-view-based 3D virtual test environment, while PAI concentrates on pre-emptive defect detection and predictive quality in welding processes. A roadmap that expands to explainability, context awareness, and agent automation through EAI/CAI/AAI, along with edge inference, MES/QMS integration, and MLOps, is useful. It is effective to clearly set KPIs such as FPY, scrap rate, and detection time, and apply them as a cell-unit PoC â†’ line expansion strategy.

This document proposes an IIoT three-layer (physicalâ€“dataâ€“service) retrofit architecture (SCT-013 current sensor/vibrometer + Raspberry Pi/ESP32, MQTT/Node-RED â†’ cloud analysis) for manufacturing energy efficiency improvement, and provides model selection criteria by problem type, such as LSTM/GRU for short-term power demand forecasting and Deep Learning/ANN for machine tool cycle energy and anomaly detection. Samsung and NVIDIA's 'AI Factory' (50,000+ GPUs) presents a large-scale reference architecture that simultaneously achieves process simulation, yield and schedule optimization, and improved equipment utilization through digital twins and predictive maintenance. AI-based collaborative robots utilize multimodal data such as gaze, movement, and biosignals to provide real-time speed adjustment, step guidance, and error prevention (E-Poka Yoke) to neurodiverse workers, including autistic adults, and enhance on-site suitability through Co-design tailored to Self-Determination Theory (autonomy, competence, relatedness). The pilot will be primarily applied to variable assembly/inspection, onboarding/upskilling lines, and mixed-model production, managing 1st pass yield, cycle time coefficient of variation, error/rework rate, line stoppage frequency, stress/load indicators, and retention rate as key KPIs, and should simultaneously incorporate ISO 10218/TS 15066, data privacy, MES/QA integration, and change management and training. Through this, alleviating labor shortages, improving quality consistency, shortening onboarding periods, and strengthening an inclusive employment brand can be simultaneously achieved.

Researchers including Virginia Tech are developing AI collaborative robots to help neurodiverse personnel, including autistic adults, work more effectively in manufacturing sites. Based on Self-Determination Theory (autonomy, competence, relatedness), it provides real-time customized feedback utilizing sensor data, and implements Human-Robot Collaboration (HRC) tailored to on-site requirements through participatory design. Manufacturers can expect practical effects in shortening onboarding, stabilizing quality, and alleviating labor shortages if they start a pilot in variable assembly/inspection processes and jointly manage indicators such as FPY (First Pass Yield), cycle time variation, error rates, and stress indicators. Key points for success include early design of safety standard compliance, multimodal UI, data governance, and MES/QA integration.

ë³´ê³ ì„œëŠ” ì—ì´ì „íŠ¸í˜• AIê°€ MCPì™€ ì˜¤í”ˆì†ŒìŠ¤ íˆ´ì„ ì˜¤ì¼€ìŠ¤íŠ¸ë ˆì´ì…˜í•´ ì •ì°°Â·ì·¨ì•½ì  ì•…ìš©Â·ìê²© ì¦ëª… íƒˆì·¨Â·ì¸¡ë©´ ì´ë™Â·ë°ì´í„° ìœ ì¶œì˜ 80â€“90%ë¥¼ ììœ¨ ìˆ˜í–‰í•œ ì‚¬ë¡€ë¥¼ ì œì‹œí•˜ë©°, OT/IT ìœµí•©ê³¼ ê³µê¸‰ë§ìœ¼ë¡œ ì—°ê²°ëœ ì œì¡° í˜„ì¥ì´ ë™í˜• ê³µê²©ì˜ ìš°ì„  í‘œì ì„ì„ ê²½ê³ í•©ë‹ˆë‹¤. ì œì¡°ì‚¬ëŠ” AI ì•…ìš©ì„ ì „ì œë¡œ ë„¤íŠ¸ì›Œí¬ ì„¸ë¶„í™”Â·ìµœì†Œê¶Œí•œ, ì—ì´ì „íŠ¸/MCP í˜¸ì¶œ ë° ë¹„ì •ìƒ ìš”ì²­ë¥  ëª¨ë‹ˆí„°ë§, SOC ìë™í™”(ë¡œê·¸ íŠ¸ë¦¬ì•„ì§€Â·ì·¨ì•½ì  ìš°ì„ ìˆœìœ„Â·í”Œë ˆì´ë¶ ìë™ ì‹¤í–‰)ì™€ ì¸ê°„ ìŠ¹ì¸Â·ê²€ì¦ ê²Œì´íŠ¸ë¥¼ ê²°í•©í•´ í™˜ê°Â·ì˜¤íƒì„ ì œì–´í•´ì•¼ í•©ë‹ˆë‹¤. ë”ë¶ˆì–´ ê³µê¸‰ë§ ì „ë°˜ì˜ AI ì‚¬ìš© ê°€ì‹œì„±Â·ì†ë„ ì œí•œì„ ìš”êµ¬í•˜ê³ , MITRE ATT&CK/ICS ê¸°ë°˜ ë ˆë“œíŒ€Â·DLPÂ·OT ì´ìƒíƒì§€ ë“± ë°©ì–´ì  AIë¥¼ ë„ì…í•´ MTTD/MTTRì„ ê³µê²©ì AI ì†ë„ì— ë§ì¶”ì–´ ì¬ì„¤ê³„í•˜ì„¸ìš”. The report presents cases where agent-based AI autonomously performed 80â€“90% of reconnaissance, vulnerability exploitation, credential theft, lateral movement, and data exfiltration by orchestrating MCPs and open-source tools, and warns that manufacturing sites connected by OT/IT convergence and supply chains are prime targets for homogeneous attacks. Manufacturers, assuming AI exploitation, must implement network segmentation and least privilege, monitoring of agent/MCP calls and abnormal request rates, SOC automation (log triage, vulnerability prioritization, automatic playbook execution), combined with human approval and verification gates to control hallucination and false positives. Additionally, they should demand AI usage visibility and rate limiting across the entire supply chain, and introduce defensive AI such as MITRE ATT&CK/ICS-based red teaming, DLP, and OT anomaly detection; they must also redesign MTTD/MTTR to match the speed of attacker AI.

The report analyzes a cyber espionage operation where AI autonomously performed most tasks from reconnaissance to data exfiltration, and emphasizes that it simultaneously targeted key industries, including manufacturing (e.g., chemical manufacturing). With open-source tools + MCP orchestration, the 'speed and scale' dramatically increased, and human intervention was limited to 10-20% for tasks like approval. Manufacturers must, reflecting the premise of AI misuse, implement network segmentation, MCP/agent call visibility, abnormal high-speed request detection, credential control, and enhanced DLP, along with SOC automation and AI-based detection/response. However, considering the possibility of AI hallucination, combining stage-by-stage human approval gates and policy-based controls is a practical key.

NVIDIA's strategy to directly control key thermal management components like cold plates and rack integration in its next-generation AI server ('Vera Rubin') demands manufacturers raise their standards for quality, performance, cleanliness, and traceability to the level of direct OEM approval. To prepare for demand in the second half of 2026, practical action points include early preparation for material procurement, equipment expansion, process automation, quality systems (helium leak testing, ultra-pure cleaning), and rack-level validation responses.

This trend article demonstrates, through industry-wide examples and figures, that AI substantially improves productivity and quality. Additionally, a strategy that connects production data with ESG indicators to quantify and optimize sustainability performance is effective.

The 'AI and Advanced Manufacturing Belt' pursued by Samsung is a model that simultaneously enhances supply chain resilience and R&D/mass production synergy by distributing and connecting national AI computing centers, regional AI data centers, and production bases for FC-BGA/IT OLED/all-solid-state batteries. From a Manufacturing AX perspective, the key is to prioritize field-edge/HPC-cloud data pipelines and security design, link energy and thermal management KPIs (PUE, kWh per process) with quality and yield KPIs, and promote the internalization of AI vision/control (EAP, SPC, predictive maintenance) in packaging, OLED, and battery processes. Furthermore, it is necessary to simultaneously strengthen the partner ecosystem and manage lead time risks by establishing process data standardization and common AI model governance between regional bases.

Samsung aims to connect key hubs such as National AI Computing Centers, Regional AI Data Centers, FC-BGA substrates, 8.6th-generation IT OLEDs, and all-solid-state batteries into a single â€˜AI and Advanced Manufacturing Beltâ€™. This provides an opportunity to accelerate process data learning, digital twin, and predictive maintenance by utilizing high-performance computing resources close to manufacturing sites, and to increase production efficiency through energy and thermal management optimization. For packaging expansion, addressing bottlenecks in responding to AI server demand is key; for new OLED lines, it's about applying AI for initial yield stabilization; and for all-solid-state batteries, it's establishing a data system for pilot-to-mass production scale-up. Manufacturing companies can set data pipeline/security, the linking of energy KPIs (such as PUE and process kWh) with quality KPIs, the internalization of specialized AI models, and data standards and governance with local partners as priority tasks.

Industry analysis indicates that soaring memory prices and the shift in production towards HBM are tightening the supply of general DRAM/NAND, putting pressure on the costs and schedules of AI servers, ESS, and automotive electronics. From a manufacturing AX perspective, practical responses include: 1) lowering procurement risks through LTA, price indexing, VMI, safety stock, and diversification; 2) shortening engineering lead times through memory optimization, expanding alternative part AVL/PPAP, and BOM locking; and 3) absorbing financial and operational shocks through S&OP scenarios, TCO recalculation, CapEx phasing, and customer unit price renegotiation. Reducing the memory footprint of AI workloads and adjusting priorities, along with resequencing schedules and costs for ESS/automotive projects, are particularly important.

To achieve AI autonomization of manufacturing supply chains, five key foundations must be implemented through a phased roadmap: a real-time visibility data architecture, security- and scalability-centric cloud-native orchestration, embedded ML within workflows, execution layer robotics/automation, and ethics and risk governance. Employ a hybrid talent strategy by integrating AI Product Managers, Data Translators, and Prompt Engineers with S&OP and planning organizations. Clarify the roles, responsibilities, and escalation procedures between humans and algorithms to leverage human strengths in areas like handling exceptions and customer communication. Furthermore, regularize digital twin-based scenario planning and manage the ROI of pilot-to-scale deployment using KPIs such as MAPE, OTIF, inventory turnover, planner productivity, and lead time. Select partners with proven technological capabilities and operational reliability.

Three major trendsâ€”AI, fragmentation, and inflationâ€”are redefining manufacturing AX strategies. As a rapid increase in AI infrastructure CAPEX and worsening bottlenecks in power, GPUs, and land are anticipated by 2026, manufacturers need to secure power through long-term PPAs or on-site generation, prioritize AI tasks considering inference costs, and pre-contract HPC and edge computing. Global fragmentation demands supply chain localization and diversification, and access to critical resources and energy becomes key to competitiveness. In an environment of increasing inflation volatility, CPI/PPI-linked contracts, investments in real assets and efficiency, and shock-absorbing designs for inventory and working capital are essential. Utilizing an AI bubble assessment framework to manage investment speed and scale, and accelerating the timing of transition investments with diverse funding sources such as private credit is advantageous.

Since the risks faced by telecommunication companies determine the connectivity, availability, and security of smart factories, design for production continuity with multi-operator/multi-path design, private 5G-public network hybrid, and SLAs focused on latency, jitter, and MTTR. Premising on edge proliferation, link Zero Trust and responsible AI governance with supply chain contracts (including data sovereignty, model security, and observability APIs), map geopolitical and regulatory exposure to proactively secure alternative suppliers and backhaul/equipment redundancy, and internalize NaaS, network slicing, and AIOps capabilities. Furthermore, metricize energy efficiency (kWh/GB), equipment circular economy, and TPRM, and reflect the disclosure of telecommunication partners' Net Zero roadmaps in RFP evaluation criteria to simultaneously achieve ESG and operational efficiency.

Assuming DRAM/NAND tightening due to HBM transition, manufacturers should re-evaluate their 2025 AI and Smart Factory budgets (with Â±20-40% price scenarios) and hedge memory lead time/price risks through advance purchases, long-term supply contracts, and multiple vendors. From an infrastructure perspective, lighten edge/server memory requirements (through quantization, pruning, KV cache), lower TCO through redesign based on DDR5/LPDDR5X and QLC and cold data tiering, and concurrently plan power and cooling facilities that reflect HBM's power and thermal load. Furthermore, it is effective to continuously monitor the roadmaps of Samsung, SK Hynix, and Micron, as well as HBM/DDR5 yield and packaging risks, and pre-validate scalability through CXL/memory pooling PoCs.

Due to soaring AI demand and a production shift centered on HBM, DRAM/NAND supply is tightening, making it highly likely that price increases will continue. This directly impacts IT/OT infrastructure costs at manufacturing sites, including AI servers, edge devices, and PCs/smartphones. Manufacturers should manage memory lead times as critical paths and prepare for long-term supply contracts, pre-purchasing, and vendor diversification. Meanwhile, lower memory/storage requirements through model lightweighting and data tiering, and review scalability and TCO with alternative spec strategies such as DDR5/LPDDR5X, QLC/TLC, and CXL/memory pooling PoCs. In data center design, re-evaluating the thermal and power budget for memory modules, alongside power and cooling, is key.

Due to the surge in AI demand, DRAM and SSD prices have risen 2-3 times in a short period, which could increase procurement costs not only for PCs and smart devices but also for industrial PCs (IPCs) and edge AI equipment used in manufacturing. Manufacturers should mitigate cost and lead time risks through multi-sourcing, long-term contracts (linked to price indices), advance ordering, redesigning and re-certifying alternative specifications, S&OP scenarios that reflect exchange rate and price fluctuations, and re-establishing safety stock levels for critical components. To prepare for SSD component swap risks, it is practically effective to strengthen firmware and performance inspection items, and to absorb budget shocks by offering spec options for memory cost-sensitive models and adjusting pilot schedules.

Explainable LLM research strengthens reliability, traceability, and auditability in manufacturing sites, providing a foundation for quantifying and reducing hallucination and bias risks in safety and quality-critical tasks such as quality determination, process parameter recommendation, and predictive maintenance. OpenAI's research 'Weight Sparse Transformer' introduces an attempt to better analyze LLM internal mechanisms (such as hallucination, intent deviation). Building generative AI agents in manufacturing sales and service with Microsoft 365 Copilot, Copilot Studio, and Azure AI Foundry (including Cloud for Manufacturing) can accelerate R&D/sales/service knowledge search, proposal and response drafting, and technical support decision-making. Utilize defense B2G as a 'pilot and certification lever' for the commercialization of physical AI to verify high-difficulty HW/SW integration and recover development costs, while simultaneously pursuing dual-use expansion such as logistics and safety inspections inside and outside factories. Due to Big Tech's debt-based expansion of AI infrastructure and slowing cash flow, changes in pricing and contract terms for cloud and AI services, rising power and cooling costs, and extended supply chain lead times for GPUs, power equipment, etc. are expected. Manufacturers should recalculate AI TCO, mitigate vendor lock-in with multi-cloud and hybrid edge strategies, combine this with factory power and cooling capacity planning, proactive procurement, and monitoring of vendor financial health (cash and cash equivalents, FCF, debt-to-equity ratio), and internally, pursue AI transformation with a phased capital allocation plan that accepts short-term cash flow deterioration.

Big Tech's large-scale investments in AI infrastructure, leveraging debt, are leading to a decrease in cash reserves and a slowdown in cash flow. From a manufacturing perspective, this could lead to increased volatility in AI and cloud pricing and contract structures, deepening constraints on power and data centers, and longer lead times for critical components such as GPUs, power, and cooling. Therefore, it is crucial to proactively manage cost, supply, and sustainability risks by reassessing AI adoption TCO, adopting multi-cloud/hybrid edge strategies, selecting energy-efficient models and infrastructure, proactively procuring and securing long-term contracts for critical components, and assessing vendor financial health.
